SEATTLE, May 12.â An Alaska trans

"The Government of France, -while ex
pressing its thanks for the marks of sym
pathy which have reached them from
America, informs us that Foi$ de France
and the entire island of Martinique are
still threatened. They therefore request
that, for the purpose of rescuing the peo
ple who are in such deadly peril and
threatened with starvation, the Govern
ment of the United States may send as
soon as possible the' means of transport-'
Ing them from the stricken Island. The
island of St. Vincent and perhaps others
in that region are also ' seriously men
aced by the calamity which has taken so
appalling a form in Martinique. I have,
directed the Departments of the Treasury.
of i War and of the. Navy to take such
measures for the relief of these stricken
people as lie within the executive discre
tion, and I earnestly commend this case
of unexampled disaster to the generous
consideration of the Congress. For this
purpose J, recommend that an appropria
tion of $500,000 be made, to be Immediately
available.
"THEODORE ROOSEVELT.

"Some men from sinking ships got to

but big stones were rained down and fell
like rain for a long time." , ; >. . ; .
"That cloud," continued Prudent, "was
bigger, it seemed, than' the mountain.
The fire burned the city everywhere at
once. Near me I saw only- dead men, but
on shore I saw men. and women: rushing
back aÂ»d forth amid the flames for an
hour. They would not run long.' Then

utes, but the city burned for three hours.
Then every house was finished, anil noth

smoke that it burned worse than Â¦ the

sions, one ton of ice and a full supply of

needed only quick burial. .
' A. G. Austen, a manager'of the Colonial
Bank of Barbadoes, landed at St. Pierre
with a party from the British Royal Mail
steamer Solent. He found the bank clock
stopped at some minutes before 8 o'clock.
A horse and buggy and a policeman were
in a dead group at the door. .
Careful inspection showed that the fiery
stream which so completely destroyed St.
Pierre must have been composed of pois
onous gases, which instantly' suffocated,
every one who. inhaled them, and of other
gases burning furiously, for nearly all the
victims had their hands covering their
mouths or were in some other attitude
showing that they had sought relief from
suffocation. All the bodies were carbon
ized or roasted.

With great difficulty a landing was ef

were heaps of corpses. Almost all the
faces/were downward.
The still smoking 1 , volcano towered above
the ash-covered hills. The ruins were
burning in many places and frightful
odors of burned flesh filled the air.
â¢ The city of SL Pierre stretched nearly

volcano. The houses of the richer French

Q TON, May 12.â President
\bl J Roosevelt slashed red tape
to-day with an energy that
shocked departmental law
' yers. His purpose was to
promptly dispatch relief for ' the suffer
ers of Martinique and St. Vincent, and
fo well have his Instructions been carried
out that supplies are now on their way
to the distressed islands,. and more will
immediately follow.
The collier Sterling with navy stores
Â£.nd voluntary contributions is believed
to have left San Juan for Fort de France.
The ocean-going tug Potomac will arrive
to-night at Fort de France. The Cincin
nati left Santo Domingo this morning for
the same destination. The Dixie with
570.000 worth of supplies will sail from
New York on Wednesday. The Buffalo
has been ordered to hold herself in read
iness to proceed to Martinique. Two
barges will probably start to-morrow
from Key West and Norfolk, conveying
good water to the thirsty islanders.
The importance cf President Roose
velt's action will be understood when it is
known that he acted in the face of an ob
jection of one of the members of his Cabi
net that he had no legal sanction for ex
pending public money without specific
authority of Congress. .
President Roosevelt's answer was an In
struction to Secretary Hay, which caused
that official to address this communica
tion to the Secretaries of War, Navy and
the Treasury:
"The President directs me to express to
you his wish that your departments go
to the furthest limits of executive discre
tion in the work of relief and rescue in
the afflicted islands of the Caribbean."
The President had been moved to take
this action by the dispatch from Consul
Ayme stationed at Guadeloupe, who, un
der an instruction from Secretary Hay,
had made a personal investigation of the
conditions In Martinique.

"All aft of the ship was afire and from
the land came draughts of terrible heat.
At last, when I could stand it no longer,
I sprang overboard, thinking that I might
swim to the mouth of the harbor and
thus escape. The water was almost hot
SPRINGS OVERBOARD.
"It soon became unbearably hot in. the
room, and I made up my mind that it
would be better outside. I went on deck
to find a scene which will never fade
from my mind. All about were lying the
dead and the dying. All were . covered
with hot mud and with ashes. The dying
were suffering terrible torture. Little
children were moaning for water. I did
what I could for them, but It was very
little.' I obtained water, but when it was
held, to their swollen lips they .were
unable to swallow because â¢ . of . the
ash'es which clogged their throats.
One little chap took water into ;hi3
mouth', and rinsed out the ashes, but even
then could not swallow, so badly was his
throat burned. He sank back uncon
scious and a few minutes later was dead.
"The ship rocked and I expected every
moment that it would sink. Outside I
heard a voice pleading for the door to be
opened. I recognized the voice as that
of Mr. Scott, the first officer, and I
opened the door and dragged him into the
room. When we did this the heat rushed
in blistering Thomas' face. We three and
Thompson, the assistant purser, and
sixty-eight souls, were the only ones who
escaped without serious injuries.
"Seeing the Roddam, which had ar
rived In . port soon after we anchored,
making for the Roraima, I said good-by
to Captain Muggah and swam to the
Roddam. Before I could reach her she
burst into flames and put out to sea. I
finally reached the Roraima again.'about
2:30 o'clock in the afternoon, and' later
was taken off by a- boat from the French

James Taylor, who lives in tnis
city and who was one of. the
officers of the , Roraima, the
Quebec line steamship which
was destroyed in the harbor of
St. Pierre, has given to the correspondent
of The Call this account of the tragedy of
last Thursday:
"We left Dominica for St. Pierre at
midnight on Wednesday and arrived at
our place of destination about 7 o'clock
the next morning. We experienced the
greatest difficulty In getting to port, ow
ing to the ashes which filled the- air. The
darkness was Intense and we had to grope
our way to anchorage.
"Appalling sounds were issuing from
the mountains behind the town, which
were shrouded in darkness. Thunder
seemed to come out of the air and up
from the earth. Ashes were falling upon
the deck, but not in such quantities as
to keep the passengers below. All were
watching the awful sight spread out be
fore us. Some were trying to obtain
photographs. â¢â¢[:'-.;
"Suddenly I heard a tremendous ex
plosion. Ashes began to fall thicker upon
the deck and I could see a black -cloud
sweeping down upon us. I dived- below,
and dragging with me Samuel Thoma3,
a gangway man and a fellow countryman
of mine, sprang into a room, shutting the
door to keep out the heat that was al
ready unbearable. *

ing at the University -of California
yesterday afternoon to confer de
grees upon the graduates. . President
Wheeler, presented the names of 332
undergraduate and -twenty-four
graduate students v who: had been
recommended for, such ;:honqrs by the
graduate council, and the/ Regents voted,
according to the usual form; to grant de
grees to those' named. '<tz "^

awarded the university medal as the most
distinguished student in the graduating
class. Etcheverry was a student :in the
college of civil engineering, and while*he
did not get the highest credits his work
was deemed the best. Several received
special mention for their work.

SCENES AT CLASS DAY EXER
CISES AT UNIVERSITY OF
CALIFORNIA.
BEN WEED'S amphitheater in the
woods back of the university
buildings at Berkeley was the
scene of the open-air production
yesterday by the graduating class
of '02, being a part of the "class
day" exercises.
A clever burlesque on the famous comic
opera "Robin Hood" was the presents
tion made by the students before one of
the largest gatherings ever seen in the
ampitheter. The arrangements werÂ«
perfect in detail, and the brilliantly col
ored gowns of thousands of fair visitors
formed a spectacle long to be remem
bered. Seats were provided in front of
the stage for invited guests of the grad
uates, and the general public found ac
commodations beneath . wide-spreading
trees.
The production of "Robin Hood" re
flected great credit on all concerned. The
various musical numbers were given in
superb style, and the score of the opera
was a keen satire on some of the faculty
and students of the State University.
The doings of President Wheeler ana
Recorder Sutton were burlesqued in a
good-natured manner by the students,
and the head of the university heartily
enjoyed his representation on the stage.
The many solos, choruses, dances and
merry Jests- were all encored, and the
work of Wilfred Yanke's orchestra and
the costumes were features of the proÂ»
duction.
PEESENTS A MERRY PLOT.
The plot of the burlesque of "Kobin
Hood," written by Lila McKlnne. was
set forth in the programme as follows:
Once Upon a time, a great while asoe, in
ye Berkeley forest, there was a good t'eUowe
lived, who was Robin Hood, and with him his
companions of 1S>O2. On a time they had lived
elsewhere, but they had been tormented by
divers oppressors, so knavish that all did com
plaine of them. Of these ye most churlish was
ye wicked Sutton of Southall. They strove
against them all, but this helped little or noth
ing. So that they were constrayned to travail
away. So they did goe to Berkeley Forest
toe be free.
Of a day Robin and his good fellowes did
set themselves to make merrie, howbeit they
were tolde that ye wicked Sutton of Southall
was coming on that same day to drive them
from ye greenwoode. Arid soe they did sende

Faculty Is Satirized by "Seniors" in Burlesque
of Comic Opera "Robin Hood," Which Is Cleverly
Given Beneath the Trees of University Grounds
OTCCE again a band of sturdy young
men and bright women has bidden
farewell to the halls of the State
University at Berkeley and with the
ceremonies attending "Class Day"
the graduating class of '02 yesterday
followed the custom of years in terminat
ing the days of scholastic training.
Although the day was cloudy, a large
crowd assembled under the broad oaks
of the campus and the graduating stu
dents and their friends held high revelry.
The young men and women who were to
bia good-by to their alma mater and en
ter the campaign of life were in the best
of spirits and the scene was one of the
most brilliant ever witnessed in the
grounds of the State Beat of learning.
The young women, who to-morrow will
receive their certificates from the hands
of the faculty, were gowned in white,
â¢while the graduating male students
donned white duck garments and wore
their battered class hats. The junior
classes enjoyed themselves at the com
mencement of the proceedings by play
ing "football" with the new headgear
they wore for the first time as "seniors,"
and the more battered the "hat the more
it was prized by the owner.
The "pilgrimage" of the class of '02 con
sisted of a concert under the "senior
cak;" the dedication of the oak to the
care of the class of '03, and the speeches
delivered on the steps of the various halls
of the university, each of which was vis
ited. An open air performance in the
woods was given in the afternoon and at
night the graduates danced the hours
away and made plans for the future.
VALUE OF FRIENDSHIP.
A feature of the speeches delivered dur
ing the day was the strong sentiment ex
pressed as to the value of friendship and
association begun by young men and
women during their college career. The
graduates who gpoke, and President
Wheeler also, dwelt upon this phase of
etudent life, and the class of '02 handed
ever traditions to those who will be sen
iors for the next year.
The "pilgrimage" commenced at 10 a.
m., after the concert given by a military
band. Under the boughs of the "senior
oak," Charles T. Dozier, president of the
graduating- class, dedicated the oak to
the care of the class of '03. He referred
to the good work achieved during the ex
piring college term and said that the co
operation of the students and faculty had
led to the greatest success. He conclud
ed by asking the class of '03 to be ever
true to the traditions of Berkeley, fos
tered under the "senior oak."
Chester Brown, president of the class of
'03, accepted the "oak" and pledged him
self that those he spoke for would be ever
faithful to the records of their predeces
sors, not only for the balance of their
college days, but also for the years to
follow.
Raymond Carter of the senior class en
tertained the crowd with a speech in
which he "joshed" the professors. He
then said that the graduates would re
member most the deeds of the heart and
not those of the head and bid farewell to
the "senior oak" In feeling words.
The procession of the graduates was
then formed. Ahead marched the mili
tary band, followed by co-eds, the male
graduates bringing up the rear. The first
stop was made at South Hall, where an
address was delivered by Winfield Dorn
He paid a tribute to the faculty and to
the memory of the beloved Professor Jo
eeph LÂ« Conte, whose presence was miss
ed at the exercises. The speaker also re
ferred to the value of college associations
to those about to embark in the duties of
life.
PRESIDENT WHEELER SPEAKS.
INFLUENCE OP THE CO-EDS.
On to the library the procession wended
its way and In front of the building a
vast crowd was gathered. J. M. Eshle
man made a short speech, in which he
dwelt upon the value of the "dig," or
etudy Fide of university life, and that of
co-operation and friendship. He then pre
sented President Benjamin Ide Wheeler,
who was cordially greeted and who spoke'
as follows:
Another year has passed away and again It
It my tura to speak to a graduating class. This
it a good place for you to come and meet me.
The duty of all g.-aduatlng students is to the
library of the university. We ask vour loyal
support for the library, for it is the very stom
ach of the university, and without a proper
library the university cannot exist. The librarv
Is out of all proportion to the needs of the
university. I am glad to say that I am re
ceiving contributions to the library from many
former students. One of them says that he
will increase his donation as his Income in
creases. I hope he will get rich. Here we
must have one of the great libraries of the
Â¦world. There is little that I can say. to you
row. as I will epeak my final words to the
class on commencement day. I thank you all
for your co-operation in carrying on the work
cf the university. I do not believe in fighting
or kickers. Every success in life comes from
r y-mpathy and co-operation and love. Be help
ers of the world to which you are now to enter
la earnest. You have stood loyally by me and
helped me to carry a big- burden. You have
helped me manfully and loyally, and again I
thank you. \ .

etepe of North Hall, where M. E. Deutsch
told of the pleasant time spent on the

SAN JOSE. May 12.â The New England
delegates to the women's conference at
Los Angeles were the guests of the San
Jose Woman's Club in the Hotel Vendome
this evening. Tliere was a profusion of
flowers, a fine musical programme was
rendered and light refreshments were
served.
Club Women Entertained.
Floral Festival at Sacramento.
The street fair whicn\opens at Sacra
mento to-day will attract" large numbers
of people from San Francisco, particular
ly in view of the one-way rate' of $2 50
made by the Southern Pacific for the
rcund trip, tickets being good from to
day up to and including Sunday, May 18,
returning. This is an opportunity to visit
the capital city at little expense and en
joy a pleasant outing. Sacramento is now
en fete, and her citizens are according
hearty welcome to all visitors.
The coal companies in various parts of
the region have already made prepara
tions fcr a siege. Coal train crews, tele
graph operators, switchmen, carpenters,
machinists and other employes, number
ing several thousand, not identified with
the minev/orkers' union have been laid off
until further notice. Some of the compa
nies have also brought their mules to the
surface and placed them in pasture.
President Mitchell spent a busy day at
his headquarters. He was in close com
munication with all the district leaders,
and expresed himself as well satisfied
with the way the men were responding
to the call for suspension of work. He
will leave for Hazleton to-morrow after
noon.
Absolute quiet prevailed everywhere to
day. In pursuance of the district execu
tive boards, which met jointly at Scran
ton last week, all the local unions
throughout the region met during the day
and evening and elected delegates to the
Hazleton convention. It is significant that
in either issue where the vote of a local
became public the ballot showed a de
cided majority for a permanent strike un
less the operators grant concessions. Pre
dictions are made 'that if it is desired on
Wednesday to continue the strike the
struggle will be longer and more bitter
than was that of 1900, which lasted six
weeks.
PHILADELPHIA, May 12.âMinework
ers throughout the entire anthracite coal
regions of Pennsylvania to the number of
140,000 began their struggle to-day for in
creased wages and shorter hours. Never
in the history of hard coal mining has a
tie-up been so complete, not one of the 357
collieries in the territory being in opera
tion. There is every indication for the be
lief that the suspension, which was to
cover only the first three days of this
week, will be made permanent by the
miners' general convention, which will
meet at Hazleton on Wednesday.

MEN OF BISBEE CLAMOR
FOB A MTTBDEBEB/S LIFE
Great excitement prevails among the
exiles, who have Just received word that
former President Sam's candidate for
the presidency is General Moot-Plalser.
The moderate and enlightened section of
the Haytian refugees are in favor of the
election of General Firman; the present
Haytian Minister at Paris.
KINGSTON, Jamaica, May 12.â The
many Haytian exiles are making strenu
ous efforts here to reach Hayti before a
new President has been elected. They had
arranged to charter a steamer and sail
for Port au Prince last Sunday, but the
Haytian consul here refused to grant the
exiles passports, and the steamship agent
at the last 'moment declineM the charter.
The followers of Callistheme Fouchard,
the former Haytian Minister of Finance
and a rival of General Sam in the lat
ter's candidacy for- the presidency of
Hayti, are anxious to return . to Port au
.Prince in order to take a hand in the
struggle which they expect will follow
the election of the new President.
Fouchard is determined to return Imme
diately to Hayti, and it is believed will
endeavor to do so by way of the United
States. .
Boisroud Canal was elected President
of Hayti July 19, 1876. A revolution
against him broke out in 1879, and he re
signed about July 17 of that year. He
was succeeded by General Salmon.
Former President Sam still occupies
the palace, but he will embark this even
ing or to-morrow on board a French
mail boat.
A provisional government iias been es
tablished under the presidency of Bols
roud Canal, a former chief magistrate of
Hayti. He says all the towns of the re
public are in a state of revolution. I
Port au Prince is now quiet, but fur
ther fighting Is expected this evening.
The street fighting, which lasted about
one hour, resulted in a triumph for the
opponents of the government. The troops
at the arsenal surrendered without any
resistance, and the government passed
Into the hands of the revolutionists. Only
two men were killed and three wounded.
The meeting, which opened at noon, had
hardly heard the words of the President
of the House, declaring that a President
of the Republic was to be elected, when
Deputy Jeanott declared that the Con
gress did not have- the confidence of the
people. This was followed by cries of
"Vive la Revolution!" and "Aux Armes!"
and a scene of great disorder followed,
during which Congress was practically
dissolved.
Shooting in the streets began soon af
terward. -

Yukon Is Open to Travel.
VANCOUVER. B. C, May 12.-The ice
in the Yukon has broken and winter Is
ended. . The break-up occurred during- a
blinding snowstorm,-, with the mercury at
40 degrees above. â¢ The .' water rose six
feet, but little damage resulted. A flood
is expected unless the jam breaks within
twenty-four hours. The river Is now clear
from Lower Lebarge to - Big Salmon.
Steamers "laden with the freight which
has been accumulated at White Horse are
following the ice down and the first is
expected, to reach Dawson: this week. '
"WASHINGTON, May 11.â An Important
contribution to the discussion of the
Philippine government bill was made
in the Senate to-day by Foraker of Ohio.
He maintained that the United States
could not leave the islands until order
had been restored and until a stable gov
ernment had been established. He de
clared that this Government would nor
come away from the islands .under j the
present ot the next administration, even
though the latter be Democratic, because
the American people would never con
sent to poltroonery. Foraker read some

which Patterson of Colorado is the pro
nrietor The editorials maintained, that
the United States must retain control ot
the Philippines and put down the insur
rection among the Filipinos. With keen
sarcasm the Senator declared that the ed
itorial did â¢ not read as did Patterson's
speeches now. Foraker held that the
policy adopted in the Philippines was
one of the kindest and gentlest possible

clared- that the minority had attacked
the army from? the commanding general
to the humblest private, all because the
taxmy had resented outrages, without

A drop of rain descended from an omin
ously dark sky, but the heavens forbore
to spoil the fairy scene, and for the rest
of the evening perfect springtime weather
prevailed. \
To-morrow will witness the big event of
the week, the floral parade. It Is expected
to start about 2:30 o'clock in the afternoon
and will contain many handsomely deco
rated turnouts.

PHOENIX, Ariz., May 12.â Charles Dieb
llng. collector and manager of the whole
sale department of Wakelln & Co.. gro
cers, to-day walked into the Sheriff's of
fice and gave himself up, confessing the
embezzlement of about $1000 of the firm's

no charge against him and he was re
leased. He is now at home with his wife
and children. The examination of his
books shows a shortage that it is believed
will aggregate $1500 or more. Rlebling
says he has been gambling, but no one
knows where.

The Condor sailed from Esquimau on
December 2d for the South Sea islands via
Honolulu and was never heard of again.
She was given up by the Admiralty and
officially written off the books on Febru
ary 17th, after some life buoys, a boat
and miscellaneous wreckage had been
found on the west coast of Vancouver
Island. It was the theory of many that
she collided with the lost Matteawan, be
lieved to have gone down in the \ same
storm, while others gave it as their opin
ion that she had foundered. The letter
from the sealer shows that the latter
theory was the correct one.
The schooner Mary Taylor is now on
her way to the Bering sea and Copper
Islands on a sealing expedition and will
be back about two months hence. "When
she arrives the scene attendant upon the
foundering of the warship may be told
in full.
It is too bad about the Condor, isn't it?
One of the schooners see her In distress on
the night of December 3. She was firing off
guns and skyrockets and afterward she went
down. It was too rough to gat near her.
The writer refers to the Condor again in
the close of his letter, saying:
I suppose you have heard all about the Con
dor, though, from the papers. â¢
This would indicate that he could give
further details of the disaster. â¢
VICTORIA, B. C, May 12.â The fate of
H. M. S. Condor has at length been defi
nitely learned. She went- down, as many
had believed, in the heavy southeast gale
of December 3d, soon after leaving the
Straits of San Juan de Fuca. The. news
of the foundering of the lost warship is
given in a letter which Robert Marshall,
a boat puller on the sealing schooner
Mary Taylor wrote to his brother, a resi
dent at Metchosin, near this city. The
letter, which is evidently the production
of a none too literate man, does not give
much detail, but this sentence in- which
lie speaks of the Condor shows that she
foundered during that awful night of De
cember 3d, carrying her crew of-Jlft, down
to death near Cape Flattery.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
The noire, smoke and flames drew a
preat crowd to the scene. Troops of chil
dren ran from their play to the fire. The
yar3s at Sheridan lie in a narrow valley.
On the pouth side of the yard Is a rounded
hill, bare of trees. On the other vide of
the yards a hill fully 200 feet high rises
sheer above the roundhouse. In less than
fifteen minutes both these hills were bfack
with men, women and children, who were
eagerly watching the flames in the cut be
low.
Suddenly a third explosion was heard,
muffled thisÂ»time, as though from a dis
tance, and eventually coming from the
mouth of the valley on the Ohio River.
Between the time when the first tank of
naphtha was damaged in switching and
pprun^r a leak and the moment when It
exploded enough of the liquid had es
caped to work its way into the sewer that
emptied into the Ohio River near the
mouth of Cork Run. Burning . oil, too
found its way into the sewer, and as soon
as it reached the open air at the mouth
an explosion followed. This third explo
sion, {separated as it was by a mile from
the i scene of the burning cars at . Sheri
servert as a warning to the few specta
tor! there. "
Men and women, their clothing ablaze,
their faces scorched and blistered and
their hair burned off their heads, ran
wildly shrieking, hither and thither, 'only
intent on escape from that awfuk furnace
of fire. Some gave vent to frenzied ap
peals to kill them and put them out of
their misery, while little children with
their curlj' locks and light summer cloth-
Ins on fire cried piteously as they were
pwept along. The townspeople did all In
their power for the stricken victims, and
the Pittsbursr ambulances were immedi
ately dispatched to the scene with a corps
of physicians. Many of those not seri
ously burned were removed to their homes
in Sheridan, while others were sent to the
various Pittsburg: hospitals. The dead
were removed to undertaking establish
ments in Sheridan and vicinity and to
the Pittsburgr Morgue.
The .first explosion sent showers of
burning naphtha upon the freight sta
tion, near at hand, and also enveloped a
number of carloads of lumber that were
close by and in a moment all were blaz
ing. Fifteen minutes after the first ex
plosion the two cars of refined petroleum,
that had been damaged by the bursting
of the tank of naphtha and were leaking,
blew up with a terrific report. No one
was injured by this, however.
A torrent of flames belched forth on
cither side of the track, sweeping back
the terrified spectators and sending a
shower of flame over their heads resem
bling Mont Pelee on a Email scale.
The scene that followed was almost be
yond description. The successive explo
sions had heated the air to such an ex
tent that before the third explosion many
were rendered unconscious by the ex
treme heat and the gaseous fumes and
were being carried away when the tor
rent of flame swept over the excited
crowd.
STJFFEKEB3 WELCOME PEATS.
The cause of the catastrophe was the
explosion "of a train of naphtha cars,
which were being switched at the yard.
In the switching the rear car telescoped
a car forward. The leaking naphtha ig
nited from a switch light, causing an ex
plosion which threw the flames fifty feet
high. Much of the escaping naphtha ran
through Corks run to Esplainborough, a
distance of one and a half miles, and
caused another explosion, blowing to
atoms the Seymour Hotel and the Collins
House, on River road, and badly wreck-
Ing a frame building near by, in which
were congregated 200 or more men from
Pittsburg and vicinity, betting on the
races and ball games. Few of the oc
cupants of this building escaped injury,
many being badly hurt. Mrs. Seymour
and her daughter of the Sheridan Hotel
were seriously and, it is feared, fatally
injured.
The first car of naphtha exploded about
4:40 o'clock, and the spectacle soon at
tracted a large crowd on the streets lin
ing the hills parallel to the railroad.
The second car exploded about 5 o'clock,
but it was 6:15 o'clock when three more
cars of the deadly stuff went up with a
roar that could be heard for miles that
the work of destruction really began.

The fight against ; : the law is to be made in ear
nest The lawyers who are handling the case have
These things will raise a laugh, but the laugh from
intelligent men- will not be long. A deliberate wrong
is .to be perpetrated in Alabama under the form ofa
farce, but the wrong is so gross and abominable that
no farcical conception of it will make fair-minded
men pass it over as. a jest.
Questions of that kind were not only intended to
do ji gross wrong,' but to make a jest while doing it.
The aim of the authors can have been nothing else
than that of turning the whole outrage into a kind
of farce and making the law itself as ridiqulous as it
is unjust. It adds to the humor of the situation that
the plaintiff who was . called upon to answer those
questions as an evidence of his understanding of the
duties of citizenship avers in his complaint that he
answered them correctly. It is a pity he did not state
what his answers were. The Monticello Club of this
city is full of bright young men who would like to
know what are the differences between the Jeffer
sonian Democracy and the Calhoun ; principles as
compared with the Monroe doctrine.
One clause of the Alabama suffrage law requires
that the applicant for registration must be able : to
understand the constitution and the duties of citizen
ship. The Registrars have a right to examine every
applicant and to reject those who in their judgment
show a lack of such understanding. The complaint
states a number of questions which the Registrars of
Montgomery County put to intelligent negroes, who
applied for registration. One of these questions was:
"What arc the differences , between the Jeffersonian
Democracy and the Calhoun principles as compared
with the Monroe doctrine?" Another was: "If the
Nicaragua canal is cut, what will be the effect if the
Pacific Ocean is two feet higher than the Atlantic?"
WHEN the telegraphic dispatches brought
information that a suit had been begun in
the courts of Alabama to test the constitu
tionality of the suffrage restrictions imposed in the
new constitution of that State little or nothing was
said of the exact nature of the complaint made by_
the plaintiff, who asked for an order of the court
compelling the Montgomery County Board of Reg
istrars to place his name upon the list of voters.
Since that time the -Atlanta Constitution has been
received containing a detailed report of the com
plaint. It reveals the fact that the Board of Regis
trars in that county at least has been carrying out
the work of suppressing the negro vote with a degree
of humor that would make the fortune of a writer of
the librettos of comic operas.

A CHANCE TO SMILE.
The project to establish such .a school should not
be forgotten in the rush of business and politics. It
should be kept in mind until the last dollar required
for its foundation has been provided. After that it
ivill be able to take care of itselt
California is in great need of trained farm workers.
The advancement of cur orchards and vineyards calls
for a high degree of skill. Men who know how to
handle fruit and vines, who understand the inten
sive cultivation required for market gardens and
berry farms, are needed in every county. Large as
have been the profits obtained by industry from our
soil, we have not yet begun to reap the full. measure
of the harvest it can be made to yield by the applica
tion of science to agriculture. The proposed school
will fit young men for that kind of farm work and
will therefore benefit the State as well as the young
men themselves, and add largely to the revenues we
derive from our farms, orchards, vineyards, gardens
and fields.
Father Crowley's project is philanthropic in its
motives, but is eminently practical in its scope and
methods. A tract of land suitable for the purpose
znd of ample dimensions has been bonded at a sum
much lower than a similar tract of land equally well
situated can be obtained in future years. Its imme
diate purchase will therefore be a profitable invest
ment to begin with. When once in operation the
industrious boys under the direction of skillful in
structors and good managers will make the farm very
near self-supporting. The enterprise is therefore
one that appeals to the business sense of the com
munity and merits support on that ground as well as
rpon the claims of benevolence.
There is an urgent need of such a school. The
drift of young men to the cities is one of the great
est evils of modern civilization. It is as bad from
an economical as from a moral point of view, for a
very large proportion of the boys who come to the
city in the hope of bettering their fortunes could
earn more, save more and have more chances to es
tablish themselves in independence by staying in the
country and making themselves masters of some
branch of rural industry.
Although the proposed school is recommended
directly by Archbishop Riordan and the task of
founding it has been undertaken by the Rev.
Father Crowley of the Youths' Directory, it is in no
sense a sectarian movement. When established the
school will make no distinction of race, color or
creed. The prime object will be that of providing
boys with a good moral training, educating them in
rseful work and fitting them to become good citi
zens, prosperous men and successful cultivators of
the soil.
Immediately upon its announcement the appeal
was received with general approval. In this busy
world, however, new things arise every day to crowd
out of mind the things of yesterday, and it is quite
probable a good many people who are willing to
contribute to the fund may have had their attention
turned to other things and are therefore likely to
overlook the proposed school. The projectors of the
enterprise are not advertising it. They are not mak
ing a popular sensation of it. Their work is being
done quietly. It is just as well, therefore, to recall
public attention to it â lest we forget.
SOME days ago there was submitted to the peo
ple of California an appeal for contributions
to aid in the establishment of a school, to train
boys in all forms of rural industry. The appeal was
supported by a circular letter, from Archbishop
Riordan, which set forth the purposes of the school,
tbe methods by which the desired object is to be
attained, and the amount of money required to pur
chase the land and erect and equip the necessary
buildings.
LEST WE FORGET.
The victory is essentially a victory for the repub
lic against all its foes. The Ministry is of a compo
site character and does not stand for any particular
party or programme, of policy except that of main
taining, the existing constitution. It would appear,
therefore, that France has at last attained stable
equilibrium and has nothing to fear from Caesarism,
reaction or radicalism. .'ââ¢:-.'
He accomplished all that without the display of
brilliancy of any kind. It has been a triumph for
plain honesty and sound common sense applied to
the. problems of statecraft. How completely he has
succeeded in eliminating the Dreyfus case from poli
tics is shown by the fact that Drumont, the leader of
the anti-Dreyfus forces, was defeated in Paris, while
Reinach, the Dreyfus champion, was defeated in the
Department of the Basses Alpes. Anti-Semitism was
routed all along the line, and the voters have
shown everywhere an intention to rid their politics
of any and every kind of racial or class antagonism.
By this victory before the people Waldeck-Rous
seau.crowns with triumph the most remarkable suc
cess that has been attained in French politics since
the establishment of the republic. When he entered
office it was said he was. but a makeshift and would
be deposed within six months. He found France
torn with dissension over the Dreyfus case, without
allies, discredited abroad and disordered at home.
By an exercise of tact, patience and diplomacy he
managed to set aside the Dreyfus case, put an end to
the agitation against the army, strengthen himself
in the Chamber, make an avowed ally of Russia and
procure for France a prestige she has not enjoyed
for many a year.
The fact that the Ministerialists were more success
ful in the reballotings than in the first elections is
in itself encouraging, for it shows that the various
elements of the. opposition are not able to combine
and that' a majority of those who oppose the Minis
try on some particular ground make it their second
choice. They will vote for their own particular can
didate on the first ballot, but if he be defeated and
the choice has to be made between a Ministerialist
and some other opponent of the Ministry they will
vote for the existing Government. That feeling puts
an end to any fears 1 concerning the stability of the
Government. So long as the Nationalists, the Im
perialists, the Royalists, the Clericals and the Social
ists will vote for the Government rather than for one
another there is no danger of revolution. The repub
lic is secure. . .
Notwithstanding some disorders here and there,
especially in Paris and Marseilles, the elections are
said to have been the quietest in the recent history
of. France. â¢, The prevalence of good order was in no
sense due to indifference, for no less than 2600 can
didates appeared for the 584 seats in the Chamber,
and the opposition to the Ministry was keen and
vigorous. The lack of serious disturbances may
therefore be taken as a. proof that the French are
learning how to hold elections without undue ex
citement and have acquired sufficient confidence in
their Government to realize it is not likely to be
overthrown however the elections result.
Out of 174 reballotings reports were received on
Monday "from 168. Of those the Ministerialists car
ried 128 and the opposition 40. As the first ballot
ings returned a Ministerial majority, it will be seen
that the Government has an assured control of the
Chamber of Deputies and the Ministry will be able
to go on with its work undisturbed.
REPORTS of the elections in France on Sun
day show that the results of the. reballotings
are even more favorable to the Ministry than
those of the first. Even in Paris, where the first con
tests gave not a single seat to the Ministry, the sec
ond gave them nineteen seats, a gain of three over
the previous â¢ Chamber, and among the. successful
candidates is M. Millerand, the Minister of Com
merce, against whom the opposition exerted all its
forces.

blundered- worse. There are; very few Americans

A tobacco chewing and : smoking con
test recently took place at the Brooklyn

eral days. to test the merits of thirty-one

The -Russian Admiralty is endeavoring
to check the extravagance and corruption
practiced by naval officers afloat and
ashore. An order has been issued for
bidding commanding officers to buy at for
eign ports any articles which are kept In
stock at Vladivostok and Port Arthur,
and purchases of articles not In store will
only be permitted on approval of the ad
miral of the fleet. Instances showing the
necessity for such an. order are given. A
commander last year paid 18 rubles a
pood for oil at Nagasaki at a time when
the volunteer fleet was only paying 4 ru
bles 80 kopeks for the same quality, and
when it could be had at Russian depots
for 1 ruble 60 kopeks to 2 rubles 35 kopeks
per pood. The commanders, likewise, are
in the habit of buying stores in foreign
ports, notably in the Mediterranean, be
fore'leaving for the Far, East at greatly
advanced prices over the stores to, be had
at Kronstadt, and their purchases are
frequently many times greater than their
requirements. This practice is to be
stopped, and the ships leaving for the
Asiatic station are henceforth to take in
a full supply of stores at Kronstadt suffi
cient for the whole .voyage.
The battleship Kaiser Karl der Grosse,
which was placed in commission January
9 last, occupied forty months in its con
struction. . The Kaiser Friedrich III, built
at Wilhelmshafen, was first of that
type of ships, and was forty-three months
under construction; the Kaiser Barba
rossa and the Kaiser Wilhelm der Grosse,
built by contract, required thirty-three
and thirty-nine months, respectively, and
the Kaiser Wilhelm II, from the Wil
helmshafen yard, took forty-one months
to , cdmplete.
The Frauenlob, a cruiser of the Gazelle
type, was launched March 22 at Bremen.
The vessel is 2600 tons displacement, with
engines . of 8000 horsepower and 20 knots
speed. She is named after one of the first
vessels in the Prussian navy of 1849, and
was presented by the Women's Society to
the Government. The original Frauenlob
was a schooner,- built at a cost of about
$12,000, and was lost with 46 officers and
men in September, 1862.
What little there is left of the Spanish
navy is. evidently not very satisfactory-
Owing to errors in the construction of the
three armored cruisers Cardenal Cisneros,
Princessa de Ashurias and the Cataluna
their bows have to be rebuilt. They were
begun eleven years ago, and it is serious
ly doubted that they will ever be com
pleted.
Two important decrees dealing" with the
organization of the French navy went into
effect April 1. The first decree relates to
the mobile defenses of the ports which are
reorganized, and the list of lieutenants
and sub-lieutenants being Insufficient,
warrant -officers will perform the duties
of commissioned officers in certain cases.
Torpedo â¢ 'boats are henceforth to go In
pairs, each boat being a support to the
other, one to be commanded by a lieuten
ant and the other in charge of a warrant
officer. â¢ The second decree affects the dis
tribution of the forces on foreign sta
tions.
The older school of officers in the Britr
ish navy is almost unanimously of the
contrary opinion to Lord Beresford in his
criticism of the navyÂ» Veterans in the
service, such as Sir Vesey Hamilton, Ad
miral -Penrose Fitzgerald and Admiral
Fremantle, have .come to the conclusion
that nothing is wrong except Lord Beres
ford. I The latter asserted in his speech
of March 14 before the London Chamber
of Commerce that:
Great Britain had no efficient naval ad
ministration, ji . / '. â¢. \ - -Â¦
Second, that the coal supply was most
unsatisfactory. -
Third, that the best admirals "did not
know how to handle a fleet. .
Fourth, â¢ that the shooting of the navy
as a whole { was lamentably defective.
Fifth, that the unbusinesslike adminis
tration of the army applied in part to the
Admiralty.
Sixth, that the training of the fleet was
defective.'
Sir. Vesey Hamilton has been First Sea
Lord of the Admiralty and commander in
chief -of more than one important naval
station, and his views, while. they may be
somewhat j conservative, are sure to re
ceive respectful attention. The two other
admirals are likewise considered able men,
but on the other hand Beresford has the
support of all the younger officers, the
bluejackets and the marines. The Ad
miralty is evidently disturbed at Lord
Beresford's arraignment, and when it was
suggested that he be disciplined, for his
utterances as being in violation of the
navy regulations, the answer was made
that the' Admiralty would pay no atten
tion "to' what a half-pay officer said. As
a member of Parliament Lord Beresford
is sure to give his : views on the navy
question, and the people will be able to
come to some conclusion as to whether
he is right or merely a sensational alarm

Nearly 30,000 men employed in the dock
yards were discharged on March 31: This
is the usual annual practice in the British
navy, at the close of the financial year.
The men are hired again the following
day, but are given new numbers in the
issue of tickets at the muster stations and
are also distributed into new gangs.
The disastrous gun accident on board
the British battleship Mars resulted in
the deaths of two lieutenants and nine
bluejackets and injury to seven others.
Oft eighteen men who were in the barbette
not one escaped either death or injury.
Two men were blown through a small
opening and only small portions of their
bodies found. Several of the wounded are
in a precarious condition- and two are
hopelessly blind. The accident is believed
to be due to a misfire, the explosion blow
ing the breech block of the 12-inch gun to
the rear, shattering the turret and seri
ously damaging the upper deck and fit
tings.

Once we had only one dependence and source of
confidence. Now we have many, and while wise mep
will always remember that rainy days will come, all
rejoice in their present capacity to provide an um
brella.
In these conditions no change is visible. The
horizon is void of any sign of surfeit. Profits and
wages are increasing; all consumption is thereby
stimulated, because ill consumption is increased.
â¢ When the Webb stocks went, tumbling and a few
brokers went with them pellmell into the ditch
men calmly turned to the day's trade report to find
an excess of orders for iron, steel and copper, and
such an excess of demand over supply that all con
tracts are underwritten, "To be filled at conveni
ence." In looking at that confidence was made as
strong as the metals which are the first indication
of prosperity and the first of hard times. The mar
ket was still clamoring for them. Its appetite was as
keen as though it had not consumed a pig or ingot,
and on their increasing strength all metal and fabric
production impinged, and men went about their
business undisturbed.
But it will be see.i that whereas credit depended
upon domestic consumption alone, standing only on
one leg, as it were, our trade is now a myriapod,
stoutly standing on many feet, and it will be under
most extraordinary circumstances that all will fail.
If we are to have a loss of confidence again and
a panic it will not be, caused by any accident or er
ror in judgment of the handlers of paper properties.
Its cause will be the ultimate gorging of our con
suming power, and the overtaking of consumption
by production. Against this the country has protec
tion in the wide reach of our/market for consump
tion. Gorging the home demand and making it list
less by indigestion will not of themselves cause a
dangerous panic, because we have access to the
consumption and demand of the world. True, these
may fail also. The power to take our food surplus
and our metal and fabric product may be also im
paired, and production here will then slacken so ap
preciably that loss of confidence and panic may fol
low.
A panic will not originate in Wall street, though
that may indicate its appearance. A thermometer
does not cause the change in temperature which it
registers.
This confidence is .born of knowledge of our
changed financial condition. To-day less of bur se
curities are held abroad in proportion to their, total
volume than at any time in* our history since we
achieved commercial importance. In 1893 such for
eign holdings exceeded two; billions, and when the
first sign of speculative trouble in Wall street was
seen. they began rushing home for liquidation. They
were payable' in gold, and. who has forgotten that
portentous drain of gold which followed? K set in
motion the greenback endless chain, draining the
treasury of its gold, until the Government had to re
sort to the borrowing of $260,000,000 of gold, which
all went into the liquidation of our securities in for
eign hands. Our foreign debt in that form to-day has
fallen far below the billion line/ Every month's trade
reduces it, and if our trade status is not changed the
next fifteen months will wipe it out altogether. Its
present volume is so small that it would hardly be
felt as an addition to any local financial difficulty. It
is as true of nations as of individuals that they know
no hard times when they are out of debt, in that form
which can be forced to immediate liquidation. We
are in that position now, thanks to the gold standard
and the vigor of our foreign trade and the unsatis
fied appetite of our domestic consumption.
THE recent flurry over the Webb stocks, in
Wall street caused some apprehension of a gen
eral panic. - This feeling, of uneasiness sub
sided when it was demonstrated that the trouble
reached only the few firms involved, and that their
fall shook none but themselves. This is the second
time in the last two years that Wall street has been
disturbed without any injurious effect upon the gen
eral credit or confidence. The speculative misfor
tunes of that financial center are viewed with indiffer
ence by the country, which goes on producing and
consuming, buying and selling, regardless of the
ephemeral troubles of those who speculate in paper
properties. . \

Special Dispatch to The Call.
SPOKANE, May 12.â 1n a room in a
small hotel at Oro Flno, Idaho, a tragic
scene was enacted some time last night.
"When the door of the apartment was
broken in early this morning one of the
occupants, a young woman, was found
cold in death, and the life of her male
companion was fast ebbing away. Both
were fully dressed, and in. the apartment
were evidences that they had spent many
hours of the night in preparing for their
tragic taking-off. On a stand were a num
ber of letters, and the man had left care
ful instructions regarding the disposition
to be made of the bodies of himself and
his companion.
The young woman was Miss Winnie, the
handsome daughter of Rev. G. M. Booth,
pastor of the Methodist Church in Mos
cow. The man, who expired soon 'after
the discovery of the tragedy, was Dr. F.
J. Leadbrooke, one of Moscow's leading
physicians and a man of wealth. They
had taken morphine by hypodermic in
jection. Evidently the physician had first
assisted the girl to end her life and had
then proceeded to encompass his own de
struction.
The prominence of the couple and the
fact that their intimacy was not suspect
ed have caused a profound sensation in
Moscow, where the girl, was reared and
where Dr. Leadbrooke practiced his pro
fession for the past six months. He was
formerly a clergyman, hailing from Min
nesota, and at various times during the
past winter occupied pulpits in Moscow.
He was a widely read man and a psycho
logical student. Dr. ' Leadbrooke had been
an Intimate friend of Rev. Mr. Booth and
his family. Miss Winnie was a handsome
young woman of 19 years and had former
ly attended the University of Idaho at
Moscow.
A few -weeks ago Misa Booth went to
Kendrlck to teach school. Her infatua
tion for Dr. Leadbrooke was not dreamed
of by her parents. On Saturday Dr. Lead
brooke left his home, saying he was
called to Kendrick. There he met Miss
Booth and . together they went to Oro

MORPHINE ENDS
GUILTY ROMANCE
LONDON, May 12.â The Chancellor of
the Exchequer, Sir Michael Hicks-Beach,
has yielded :to the " storm of disapproval
aroused by the feature In the financial
bill affecting checks, and to-day definitely
announced In the House of Commons the
withdrawal of the extra. tax of one penny
placed on checks. He added. that he re
served the right to Impose another tax to
meet the resulting deficiency, j
On the second reading of the finance bill
Sir William Vernon Harcourt, Liberal,
moved that "this House declines to im
pose duties on grain, flour and other food
of the people." He urged that the inno
vation contravened the fundamental prin
ciple of sound finance. â¢ Â¦
The Chancellor of the Exchequer in re
piylngr denied that there had been any
general rise in the price of bread. For a
moment the demand was greater than the
supply,. but it must not be forgotten that
while It was necessiiry for Great Britain
to obtain wheat and flour from' America
and other quarters it was equally neces
sary for them to send tl*eir (products to
this country. It was absurd to suppose
that- the 3-penny duty on grain could
cause a single acre to go out of cultiva
tion In America. She must send her
wheat here on account of the protective
duties elsewhere. The production of grain
throughout the world was Increasing at a
greater ratio than the demand. ' He be
lived that the producers and carriers of
grain would pay the new tax if there was
a big crop. It was : necessary to impose
additional taxation on the masses in or
der that they should be made to realize
what war preparation and war involved.

Had liong Passage.
The bark Diamond Head arrived yesterday
from Lahalna. She was thirty days making
the passage. She brought 20,237 bass of sugar.
The Diamond Head went to Lahaina from
Puget Sound and was more than forty days
going down. She encountered very heavy
weather on the outward passage and sustained
considerable damage to her bulwarks. This
damage will be repaired during her stay In port.
Mariposa on Drydock.
The Oceanic Steamship Company's Marlposa,
which has been converted into an oil burner
and Is being fitted up for the Tahiti mall
service was docked yesterday at Hunters Point.
She will be on the dry dock several days.
When ready for sea the Mariposa will be the
equal of a new vessel and as smart a steam
ship as has ever ploughed the south seas.

A telegram was received yesterday from
Ca.Dt*ln Burnes (stating that he still had hopes

now due here from Pago Pago. The McDonald

were arrested by the commandant of the .naval
station and sent here to be tried on a charge
of mutiny. They were found not guilty and
discharged. The first and second mates of the
McDonald who came here with the prisoners,
returned to Pago Pago with a substitute crew
and the McDonald resumed her voyage. Bender,
the newly appointed skipper, has been until
recently mate of the five-masted schooner W.

Movements of Steamers.
The Dollar Steamship Company has been
complimented by the consignees of -the cargo
brought here recently from the Orient on the
steamship Arab on the condition in which the
freight was delivered. Oriental freight is
fragile at the best and the Arab's careo in
cluded all kinds. Most of It was packed In
mats, jars and cane baskets. S. L. Jones &
Co., who received the cargo, declare that there
was In all of it not a single broken package,
that not an article was in any way damaged
and that when the whole miscellaneous col
lection of Oriental merchandise was delivered
there was not a claim of any kind registered
against the ship.' The cargo included 50,000
mats of rice, and there was not a tear in
one of them. This is an unusual occurrence,
particularly with Oriental freight, and reflects
credit on the officers of the steamship under
whose care It was stowed.

Mrs. Theresa Fregulia, an Italian woman,
on her way from Italy to this city, gave birth
to a boy baby on Sunday afternoon in the
saloon of the terry-boat Berkeley. In the ab
sence of a physician Captain Jones officiated
and rave the little stranger a welcome to Cal
ifornia. Mrs. Fregulla was accompanied by
her husband, who has a home all ready for
her and the baby at 314 Pacific street. The
baby was born on the 5:30 trip from Oakland.
When the boat reached this side Captain Jones
notified the police. Sergeant Ellis In turn noti
fied the Harbor Hospital. There was no am
bulance there, so securing a stretcher Ellis,
with Policemen Dowd and Rand, carried mother
and son to the hospital, where they remained
until arrangements could be made for their
reception elsewhere. Both mother and son
are doing splendidly and young Fremilia is
going to have Joneeio for one of his names.
Born on. the Berkeley.
Notice is hereby given that on or about the
25th of May Columbia River light vessel No.
50 will be temporarily withdrawn from Her sta
tion oft* the entrance to the Columbia River,
and about 7% miles S.S."VY\ % W. from Cape
Disappointment Lighthouse, Washington, for
repairs to copper on her hull, and the station
marked by a gas buoy; painted red, withi
"light vessel moorings" In black letters, and'
showing a* fixed white light The vessel will
be returned to her station on or about May
28, or as soon as the repairs can be made, and
the buoy withdrawn.
By order of the Lighthouse Board.
W. P. DAY, Commander, U. 8. N.,
Lighthouse Inspector.

NEWS OP THE OCEAN".
THE American ship Dirigo, now out
10S days from Hongkong for Hono
lulu, was put back yesterday on
the overdue list and quoted at 15
per cent. The Dirigo was spoken
on March 30 off one of the Japanese isl
ands and with the report of the speak
ing was taken off the overdue list. The
rate on the British ship Tweedsdale, now
out 125 days from Hongay to Puget
Sound, was advanced yesterday to 25 per
cent, while on the Alexander Black and
the Santa the rate was reduced to 10 per
cent
The H. P. Glade, now out 205 days from
this port for Liverpool, still stands at
SO per cent, and despite her lengthy pass
age a large sum of money is being placed
on the chance of safe arrival.
tug Astoria failed to stir on Saturday night,
and that another attempt would be made the
next srwine tide.

Young Peter Jackson and Al Weinig
k entered the ring:, challenging: each other
[and were matched to box at rort Erie
in the near future. Both men wore
bandages on their hands and both seemed
in fine form.
It was announced that Gans weighed in
at 12334 and Erne at 132%. The weight for
the bout was 136 pounds at the ringside.
The men shook hands at 9:49.
Round Iâ They came out and sparred
briskly. Gans led left for face twice and
missed. Gans led with both bands for
face. Erne ducked and they clinched.
Again Erne tried the left. Gans side
stepped and a slight mix-up followed,
Gans having the better of the bout. Gans
sent a right to the jaw. In the exchange
Gans got both hands to the head and
Erne seemed a trifle dazed. Gans put a
left to the nose, drawing blood. Erne
seemed dazed and Gans rushed and ex
changed, putting a right plump on Erne'tj
jaw. Err.c fell slowly to the floor, with
his mouth and nose bleeding, rolled over
on his stomach and was counted out. The
time of the round was one minute and
forty seconds.
over the defeat. It took a minute to bring:
him back from dreamland and there were
tears in his eyes when he realized his
defeat.
There was scarcely a seat left in the
house, which has a capacity of 5000.
There was much betting on the bout,
several thousand dollars being placed,
with Erne favorite at 100 to I*o and 100 to
SO.
Gans entered the ring at S>:3o, followed a
few minutes later by Erne. Gans crossed
the ring and shook hands with Erne who
got a great ovation.
Art Sims, Kid Parker, George McFad
den, Kid McPartland and all the best
lightweights of the country challenged
the winner.
"Of course," he said, "I did not expect
to win so quickly, but I believe the end
would have been the same had the fight
gone much farther."
Hereford Eaid he would defend the
lightweight championship at the light
weight limit. Erne was broken-hearted
The end came -with startling; sudden
ness. The men were scarcely warmed up
when Gans, trying cautiously, caught
Erne napping, and landed a hard right
en the ear, which appeared to jar Frank
gladly. Erne sparred^ wildly and Gans
*ent out a left jab as a feeler. It went
home through Erne's guard, drawing
blood from his nose. Then Gans rushed
in. Erne's defense was pitiably weak and
Gans slapped left to the face and right to
the point of the jaw. The blow complete
ly settled Erne- He sank slowly to the
floor, fell on his back and was rolling
over on his stomach when Referee White
finished counting him out. The quick de
feat astounded the hundreds of Erne men
at the ringside. The Hereford party was
overwhelmed with congratulations and a
crowd of negroes proudly escorted Gans
to his dressing; room.
RINGSIDE. FORT ERIE. Ont.. May 12.
Joe Gar.s, the Baltimore colored light
weight pugilist, is champion in his class,
atter knocking at the door for the past
ten years. Frank Erne lost to him in the
first round to-night at Fort Erie.

REDDING, May 12. â Coroner Thomas
Greene has returned from- Sallee, whither
ho went to hold an inquest upon the body
of George Rodgers, who was killed in the
blowing up of the Bastian Hotel by dyna
mite on Friday night. The Coroner and
his Jurors made the closest possible in
vestigation into the explosion and the fire
which followed. The jury set aside all
theories of accident, finding that Rodgers
died bv suffocation in -a fire caused by
dynamite set off by unknown persons
with murderous intent.

Judge Cabaniss rendered his decision
yesterday morning on the motion to dis
miss the case of W. C. Butts, . charged
with an assault upon Thomas Garrett. He
said he had carefully considered the tes
timony offered, but could find no evidence
whatever connecting the defendant with
the assault except the statement by Gar
rett that he was positive he had seen the
face of Butts at or about the time of the
aftault, but he was not prepared to say
as to the Â¦ time and. place. The Judge
therefore considered It his duty to dismiss
the case.

Praises Wife in Will.
The will of Henry T. Holmes of the
Henry T. Holmes Lime Company, who
died April 28, was filed for probate yes
terday. He leaves his estate, the value
of which is unknown, to his widow,
Laura Virginia Holmes, to whom he
says in the document "he owes every
thing." In a codicil he names E. H. K.
Hawkins as executor.

The remains of the deceased father
will be brought to the church from the

The Rev. Henry Imoda died at St. Ig
natius College yesterday in the 71st year
of his age and the fifty-second of his
religious life. Born in Turin, Piedmont,
he went, when still quite young to Naples,
where he afterward began his classical
studies and laid that broad found
ation in classical scholarship which later
distinguished him. He was received into
the society in 1850. On the completion of
his literary studies he was sent to Bene
vento, where he taught the classics for
eome years. The orders of his superiors
next brought him back to Naples, where
he took up his theological studies, and on
their completion was ordained priest. In
1S67 he was ordered to start for California
and thence proceed to the great north
west in order to work among the Indian
tribes. On his arrival in San Francisco,
however, it was decided that California
was to be his field of labor, and he was
sent to fjanta. Clara College to act as
bursar, a position which he held for nine
teen years. In 1S89 he became president
of St. Ignatius College, this city, and on
the conclusion of his term of office was
appointed superior-general of the society
in California. He held this office for five
years, when ill health necessitated his
resignation. He possessed many gifts of
mind and heart, and the present college
library is a monument to his industry and
scholarship.
lege Passes A-way After Years
of Earnest Work.

DIES AT ADVANCED AGE
Judges Gilbert, Ross and Morrow, sit
ting as the United States, Circuit Court of
Appeals, handed down an opinion yester
day that practically ends the litigation
between J. Hanley and Charles
Sweeny, F.' Lewis Clark and the Empire
State Idaho Mining and Developing .Com
pany. The suit involves Hanley's title to
a one- third interest in the Skookum min
ing - claim, - in Idaho, and the opinion of
the' Circuit Court of Appeals sustains
that title and instructs United States Dis
trict. Judge Beatty /of Idaho to issue an
injunction restraining Clark and Sweeny
from taking ore out , of the Skookum
mine, to order an accounting for $150,000
worth of ore taken out of the mine by
Clark and Sweeny and to appoint a re
ceiver. . .
The complainant alleged that Clark and
Sweeny, had represented to him that the
underground workings of the Last Chance
mine in penetrating the Skookum claim
had disclosed ground of little value and
containing.no ore. He Â¦ averred further
that, misled by this fraudulent repre
sentation, he agreed to sell his interest to
Sweeny and Clark for $10,000, and that he
afterward learned that Clark and Sweeny
had taken. ore of the value of $315,000 out
of the Skookum:'. He. therefore asked that
kthe agreement to sell be declared null and
void from its date, and for such further
relief as the circumstances might indi
cate. The case - went up . to the Supreme
Court of. the United /States and Hanley
was declared to be the owner, of one-third
interest in the Skookum and in $150,000
worth of "ore taken out of it after the
date of the, fraudulent agreement of sale.
The concluding paragraph of the opinion
of the Circuit Court of Appeals Is as fol
lows :
We ' are of the opinion that the petitioner
is entitled to have the . niandate-'of this court
in Hanley vs. Sweeny et al. enforced, but In
view of the. statement of the respondent. Judge
Beatty, . that he will proceed to enforce it
upon being advised as to the views ot inls
court upon the questions we have been dis
cussing in this -opinion we will withhold the
writ ' of mandamus until the further action
of the court.

er cases as follows:

Decree affirmed. Judge Ross dissenting.

and the American, Bonding and Trust Com
pany of Baltimore City \vs. C. W. Chamber
lain & Co. â Washington. Decree of District
Court reversed and cause remanded, with in
structions to dismiss the libel.
In re Standard Laundry Company in bank
ruptcy â San Francisco. Order of the District
Court affirmed with costs.

Captain Brown Is Dead.
Captain John B. Brown, for 35 years
keeper of the lighthouse at Point Bonita
and v one of the best known and most
popular mariners on the coast, succumbed
to an attack of pneumonia at his resi
dence in Sausalito Sunday. Captain
Brown retired from active duty a few
months ago, after making a name second
to none as a good sailor and a faithful
government servant. He was a man
than whom none braver or more kindly
existed. He was 70 years of age and is
survived by a widow. ; - - _â¢ Â¦. ...: :
Sidewalks Must Be Cleared.
Chief Wittmanv issued an order to the
different police captains yesterday calling
their attention to the manner in which
the sidewalks are being blocked by down
town merchants. The Chief has taken
measures to put a stop to the blockade
and has directed his subordinates to see
that the law is enforced hereafter and to
keep at least one-half of the blockaded
sidewalks clear for pedestrians.

The sub-committee of the Republican
Executive Committee, recently, appointed
to frame a call for a State convention,
will meet at the Palace Hotel next Satur
day afternoon.
Nobody blames Governor Gage for "stand
ing by his friends," but he must not blame
the people if they Judse him by the sort of
friends he chooses. If the Governor wants to
be a friend of W. F. Herrin, nobody 'can pre
vent, but neither can they alter or conceal the
fact that W. F. Herrin is the paid political
agent of the Southern Pacific Railroad, and
that the price of his friendship is political ser
vice to that corporation.
It weuld'not be surprising if E. H.
Harriman, president of the Southern Pa
cific, should signify directly to all the
officers, employers and attaches of the
corporation that the company had no
candidate for Governor and no sugges
tions for either convention. Herrin's in
terference in the Senatorial contest and
his open advocacy of D. M. Burns was
said to be excusable in raâ road circles on
the ground that Collis P. Huntington,
then president of the company, desired
Burns' election and so advised the com
pany's - political manager. It is doubtful
if Herrin can justify his Interference in
the present contest on the ground that
President Harriman has a candidate for
Governor whose nomination the company
prefers! The people of both political
parties would be happier and the railroad
people themselves would be better off if
the Southern Pacific Company would keep
out of politics. There is no excuse for
railroad obtrusion in this campaign. The
notion that Herrin is in politics up to his
neck Is not a delusion of the San Fran
cisco press. It is well understood
throughout the State that the attorney of
the company is a political manager. This
sort of comment from the Fresno Repub
lican is significant:
The original plan of campaign in this
State, as outlined by Burns and Herrin,
provided for the nomination of Gage by
the Republican convention and the nomi
nation of a weakling in the Democratic
party to oppose him. It was calculated
that W. F. Herrin would be able to fix
things In the Democratic camp to make
Gage's election easy. Since the first of
May a, change has come over the spirit
of the political push. All signs point to an
awakening in both political parties. It
seems that the voters have resolved to
elect the delegations to nominating con
ventions.

The sentiment of the party seema to
favor a large convention.

Two-thirds of the estate were distribut
ed last year. The remaining one-third af
fected by the order of yesterday consists
of the following properties:
Bonds of the South Pacific Coast Rail
road, worth $2,750,000; stock in the Pacific
Improvement Company. $750,000; personal
property, 5100,000; cash, $1,000,000, and prop
erty at Eleventh and Mission streets and
elsewhere of sufficient value to bring the
total of the one-third of the estate up to
57,000,000.
Judge Maguire objected to the making
of the order, but he was overruled. He
was given twenty days in which to file
b. bill of exceptions on an appeal to the
Supreme Court. All the other suits pend
ing in Judge Troutt's court in the name
of Mrs, Craven were ordered stricken
from the calendar.
The application for distribution was
made by Charles Wheeler, attorney for
Virginia Fair Vaxiderbllt. C. S. Neal, who
has managed the estate, testified that the
property was in condition to be turned
over to the heirs, Charles Fair, Mrs. Van
derbilt and Mrs. Oelrichs, within twenty
four hours.
An order for the distribution of the es
tate of the late James G. Fair was made
by Judge Troutt yesterday. In making the
order the claims of Mrs. Nettie R. Cra
ven were settled, for it is a formal
declaration that the woman who has
fought for a share of the Fair estate for
more than seven years has no standing as
c claimant of the Fair millions.
Miss I. E. Coles of the South End Pri
mary School and Daniel Whitehead, who
was one of Miss McKinney's pupils in the
John Swett Grammar School, also testi
fied. The trial will be continued next
Monday} morning. ; â¢ ; ,-' :t "
Following Lyser's .witnesses a number
of the principals of the various grammar
schools wherein Miss McKinney had
taught, and who had been called by the
board of its own accord were examined.
Miss K. Conklin, principal of the Star
King School, was the only one. who di
rectly asserted that Miss McKinney did
not do her work in a competent manner.
Mrs. N. A. Wood, principal of the Hearst
Grammar School, Mrs. M. Ifurphyj princi
pal of the Irving M. Scott Grammar
School, and Philip Prior of the -Burnett
Grammar School, but who taught in the
James Lick Grammar School when Miss
McKinney was there, agreed that while
they could find very little or no fault with
Miss McKinney's work in the classroom,
they did not desire to have her as a
teacher under them as she appeared to
be of an unhappy disposition and that she
lacked cordiality.
When the trial was commenced in the
afternoon Lyser called to the stand Mrs.
J. H. Erkson, Miss E. B. Brooks and
Miss M. C. Barry, all of whom were
teachers in the John Swett Grammar
School during the time that Miss McKin
ney was employed there as an instructor.
They were summoned to substantiate the
charges preferred by Lyser and they all
agreed that Miss McKinney was not con
genial, and she did;not affiliate with tne
teachers, but none of them would assert
that she was incapable of performing her
duties as a teacher. Their testimony for
the* most part dealt with matters of triv
ial importance and was not in" the main
unfavorable' to the accused teacher.
"Miss McKinney was suspicious of her
associate teachers, whom she said were
conspiring against her," said Lyser. "She
made complaints against Miss Barry and
Mrs. J. H. Erkson, teachers in the John
Swett School. On investigation I found
the charges to be witnout foundation. Her
conduct was that of a person unfit to
teach, although she was at all times neat
and industrious." â¢
The trial of Miss" Mary McKinney on
charges of incompetency, preferred by
Principal Lyser of the John Swett School,
began yesterday morning before the
Board of Education. Proceedings were
delayed for a time owing to the absence
of Superintendent Webster. When word
was sent to Webster to attend the trial
that official sent word that he must de
cline to take part in the hearing, as his
duty in the case had ended when he made
a report on his preliminary investigation
of the charges.
Lyser was the first witness called' and
was on the stand for two hours during
which he reiterated the c-arges against
Miss McKinney filed in a written com
munication with the board. Lyser dis
claimed that he had declared that Miss
McKinney was mentally incompetent.
Lyser, however, testified that her- mind
was not in a normal condition. He found
her to be nervous and excitable. 'Â¦â¢
Testimony Offered Is Not Al
together Unfavorable
to Accused.

PARK CITY. Utah. May 12.â "Dummy"
Rowan of Park City and "Deafy"

police have in custody three women and
three men, two of the male prisoners be

ups In this city. The women had been
consorting with the men.

Mr. Levy," said Mrs. Miller, "but I am
sure it was not a serious friendship. He
never spoke to her of love and I looked
upon him as simply one of her young gen
tleman friends, as I do upon others who
call here at the house to spend a pleasant
evening. He seemed to be a very good
young man, and I cannot imagine why he
should have taken his own life."

and mother were prostrated with grief.

WASHINGTON, May 12.â Joaquin Ber
nardo Calvo, the Minister from Costa
Rica at Washington, -issued to-day the
following statement : , .
The news from Panama published to-day to
the effect that the military element In the
neighborhood of San Jose, Costa Rica, Is in
insurrection, is incorrect. There is no insur
rection at . any place in that country. Presi
dent-elect Esquivel was inaugurated on the
8th instant, according to law. and the news of
this fact was officially communicated to the
legation ot Costa Rica, at Washington on the
8th instant, everything being quiet there as
usual. - "
The assertion that the Insurrectionists are
proclaiming a leader that does not - want to
lead explains In itself that the report from
Panama Is Incorrect.
The Important changes that had been made
among the Government officials, and especially
in the Cabinet, are facts simply consequent to
the change of administration, as It occurs else
where.
There is no Insurrection in Costa Rica.
Says That There Is No Trouble at
" Anv Place in Costa
Rica.

RENO, New, May 12.â Wells, Fargo &
Co. and the railroad officials along the
line of the Southern Pacific through Ne
vada and the western portion of Utah are
excited over the appearance of several
bands of suspicious travelers, supposed to
be train robbers, and have taken precau
tions to guard their property. Detective
John Thacker and a number of other se
cret officers were sent from San Francisco
and are watching each train as it passe3
through the territory in. which the sup
posed bandits have been seen. Bank of
ficials in towns along the line have been
notified and have taken extra precautions
to guard their- gold. .
Several days ago three different parties
of men were noticed in the vicinity of
Beowawa. They were first observed in
the northern portion of the county and
traveled in separate bands, heavily
armed, mounted on exceptionally flna
steeds and led pack horses. Their actions
alarmed the 'railroad men. who notified
the officials in San Francisco. A confer
ence was held by the railroad's agents
and those of the express company. De
tectives were dispatched to Nevada on a
special train and at once placed guards
on every train in transit over the desert
territory. Every car has been provided
with men armed with Winchester rifles,
and detectives have been stationed at
points along the route to watch the move
ments of the mysterious travelers.

SECOND MONEY AT WORTH
Robert Fitzsimmons made his ultima
tum last night. He informed representa
tives of the press between acts at the
Alcazar Theater that he would give
James J. Jeffries two weeks to decide
whether he wanted to battle for heavy
weight supremacy. Lanky Bob. through
the press, invites the clubs of this city
and vicinity to put in bids for the con
test. These bids must be sealed and will
ho. opened at the Central Theater at 3
o'clock on the afternoon of May 26.
"I came 3000 miles to get a fight, and if
Jeffries will not come to terms I will re
turn, and if it is possible will declare my
self champion of the world. 1 want Jef
fries to talk for himself, and I'll do my
own talking. Clark Ball, my manager, is
here, but when it comes to arranging a
championship battle 1 will make the ar
rangements. Then why should Jeffries
leave it to third parties"/ Delaney may be
all right as a trainer, but I won't have
anything to do with him. I want Jeffries
to put up ?23u0 as a guarantee that he will
accept the highest offer. My money is up
and has been up for some time."
