CITY COUNCIL EXONERATES
ACCUSED C0MMISSI0NEP.S
Denounces as Baseless the Charges
Preferred Against ,San Jose
Officials.
SAN JOSE. May 12.â Police Commis
sioner Andrew S. Mackenzie and his col
leagues were to-night found not guilty of
the three -remaining charges of extortion
and blackmail preferred by Kenry E. Mil
ler. On Friday night. Mackenzie was ac
quitted of the charge that he used his
official position to throw city contracts to
a foundry he was alleged to have owned.
The hearing on the third charge, that ths
Commissioners extorted theater passes
from the manager of the Victory Theater,
was set for this evening. The other two
charges were that firemen were assessed
for political purposes and that gamblers
paid protection money to John D. Mac
kenzie with the knowledge of the Com
missioners. As announced In the docu
ment filed Saturday, stating that Attor
neys Hardinge and Firebaugh would
withdraw and alleging partiality on the
part cf the Council, the lawyers did not
appear. Miller also was absent.
Attorney Cothran, representing the com
mission, demanded a hearing. He de
nounced the withdrawal of Miller and his
<cunsel as cowardly, and said he himself
had been attacked because he saw reason
to take the case of the Commissioners.
Commissioners Andrew S. Mackenzie,
Lion, Osterman, Carmichael and Dreisch
ireyer all took the stand and 'pronounced
the charpes absolutely false. The exam
ination of these witnesses occupied but a
few minutes, and they were merely asked
whether the charges were true "or not.
The Council then retired and brought in
a resolution declaring the Commissioners
not guilty. It is stated that no proof had
been offered to sustain any charge made.
Suddenly a puff of wind caught the ship
and whirled it over the Avenue du
Maine. The ship turned completely
around and veered about in a manner
that showed control- of her had been lost.
Then from the center of the .outer en
velope a tongue of flame darted out.
This was followed by a loud report, re
sembling a cannon-shot, and the ship
sank like a stone, falling on the roof of
a house, thence into some trees and
finally reaching the Avenue du Maine,
almost on top -of Its occupants, who had
previously been precipitated to the stone
pavement. The frightfully mangled
corpses of Severo and Sachet were con
veyed to a neighboring police-station.
Practically not a bone of either victim
was left unfractured. Sachet had been
shockingly burned by the explosion.
Santos-Dumont, who has been confined
to his bed for three days suffering from
nervous trouble, said to-day:
"I do not want to talk about Severo.
as I did not see the disaster. I have not
read the accounts in the papers and only
know what my friends have told me.
From what Lachambre, the builder of
Severo's and my balloons, says, I think
Severo lost his head. I understand he
threw out ballast as the airship was ris
ing rapidly. This, if true, is sufficient to
explain the accident. Rapid rising would
drive out the gas through the valve and
send it against the motors, thus causing
an explosion. One defect of Severo's air
ship was that the motors were too near
the balloon.
"No. the catastrophe does not discour
age me, but I fear there will be a set
back to aerial navigation. It will frighten
many persons and discourage those who
wen? Inclined to spend money in such un
dertakings."
PARIS, May 12.â The death of Senhor
Severo, the Brazilian aeronaut, who with
his assistant was killed this morning by
the explosion of his airship La Paix, in
which he was making a trial trip, created
terrible consternation.
1 Senhor Severo had invited many friends
to witness the ascension, and his wife and
a number of relatives were following the
course of the balloon in motor cars. Sud
denly the spectators were horrified by a
bright Hash of light, followed by a loud
explosion. The balloon, which at this
time was 1500 feet above the ground, fell
rapidly, landing on the roof of a house.
The aeronaut fell into the Avenue du
Maine and was dashed to pieces. The
bones of his legs were forced through the
soles of his boots. The engineer who ac
companied him was burned to death.
The accident is said to have been
caused by a leakage of gas.
Senhor Severo started with the intention
of sailing to the military parade grounds
at Issy, and a large number of his rela
tives and friends were proceeding In the
direction of that village, situated five
miles southwest of Paris, and thousands
of others were watching the flight. With
an engineer named Sachet, Severo sailed
out ot his balloon house near the Boule
vard de Vaugirade at 5:30 a. m. In excel
lent spirits and full of confidence, the
aeronaut put his airship through a series
of evolutions before, starting in the direc
tion of Issy. There seemed to be some
hitch with the steering gear, and the rear
propelling screw turned with difficulty.
Nevertheless, after several stoppages, the
airship sailed off. moving steadily enough
through a light breeze.
Deprivation of his sense* of smell
prompted P. J. Keller, known in politics
as the "Mayor of Piedmont," to sue the
Oakland Transit Company for damages,
alleging that that corporation's negligence
was responsible. Keller set a price of $23S
his olfactory nerve, but he did not get
even a part of that sum, for when the
case came up to-day before Judge Hail
the court decided that he was guilty of
contributory, negligence.- -
The accident that put Keller s sense of
smell out of business occurred one night
two years ago, while he was returning
from a political meeting in East. Oak
land. He was thrown off a Transit Com
pany car at Eleventh- and, Harrison
streets and landed on his head on the
pavement. His wounds dressed at
the' Receiving Hospital, Wit when he re
covered he found that he couldn't smell a
thingâ not even the concentrated odors of
Chinatown on a wet night.
Keller tried to have the maltreated
nerve readjusted, and he consulted some
good doctors, but they couldn't make
things odoriferous to him again. So he
began the suit that ended so disastrously.

The burning of this depot removes the
last trace of this gigantic town scheme
that failed. Had it not been for this
scheme the narrow-gauge road would
have gone through Centerville, one of the
oldest and most thriving settlements in
the lower part of this county, but which
is now handicapped by being two miles off
the railroad.
The big depot building at Newark, on
the narrow-gauge line to San Jose, was
burned this morning at daybreak. There
are no fire-fighting facilities at Newark
and all that the inhabitants of the little
town could do was to prevent surrounding
buildings from being burned.
The fire was discovered by the watch
man, who gave the alarm. The people or
Newark turned out and some came down
from Centerville, among them Supervisor
Homer. Mr. Horner organized a flre
flghting brigade, but having no water, the
burning building was pulled down and
surrounding structures were thus saved.
With the burning of this â¢ depot disap
pears the last mark of the effort that
was made by the late James G-? Fair and
A. E. Davis to make Newark the ter
minus of .their railroad system and a rival
to Oakland. When the narrow-gauge sys
tem was flrst built it was intended that
Newark should be the terminus of the
road. Newark is at the land end of Dum
barton Point, which projects into San
Francisco Bay, â¢ and the narrow-gauge
road originally ran to the end of this
point. There ferry slips were built and
the ferryboat Newark was built to run
from Dumbarton to San Francisco in an
hour. Car shops were built and this big
terminal depot constructed of sufficient
size to accommodate all the* travel that
could arrive, even over an overland line-
Town lots were sold and Newark was to
become a great railroad and manufactur
ing center.
But it was found that the steamboat
Newark, though it was the fastest on the
bay, could not make the trip in the hour,
and so Senator Fair was compelled to
build his road into Oakland and Alanteda
and the big depot at Newark stood there,
almost deserted, covering the tracks that
were once supposed to terminate there,
but had to be laid on to the city that
Newark was to rival.

WEALTHY MERCHANT
. GOES TO LAST REST
Nathan Goldtree of San Luis Obispo
Dies in City After Long
Illness.
, Nathan Goldtree,'*y native of Germany
and a well known banker of San Luis
Obispo county, died on Sunday night at
vjjis residence, 930 Eddy street in this city,
at the age of 64 years.
Mn.Goldtree came to California in 1S58
and settled in San Luis. Obispo County,
where he engaged in merchandising and
banking and made a fortune. He estab
lished a branch of his business in â¢ Salva
dor and the firm has offices in that city,
San. Luis Obispo and elsewhere. He was
very generous in his charities and was
one of the most popular men In Central
California and his loss will be deeply felt
by hosts of friends outside his family and
relatives. " . â¢'
The deceased leaves a wife, two. sons,
Morris and Sidney, a married daughter,
Mrs. L. Blockman of San Diego, and, one
unmarried. daughter, Miss Palmyra Gold
tree. The interment will be private and
the body will be laid in the Hills of Eter
nity Cemetery.' Â¦ â¢ â¢ Â¦. . . '.

Barton Hospital Be Declared
Mrs. Jennie Tibbetts Asks That Clara
a Nuisance.
Jennie Tibbetts, who keeps a lodging
house at 504 Geary street, brought a suit
yesterday against the Clara Barton Hos
pital, which adjoins the lodglhg r house, for
$10,000 damages. She also asks . that tne
tiospital be declared a nuisance.
Mrs. Tibbetts alleges thaj the hospital
exists in violation of a city ordinance, and
is a cause of woe to her because of the odor
Of disinfectants and the cries and moans
of the patients. She alleges that the fre
quent calls made upon the place by un
dertakers, who drive up with dead wag
ons, have driven a number of her lodgers
away, thereby causing her great pe
cuniary loss.
Attorney A. Ruef filed a notice yester
day that an appeal would be taken from
the decision of Judge Sloss in the Justices'
clerk case. He is the legal representative
of Powel Fredrick, who was ordered by
Judge Sloss Saturday to turn over "the
books and papers of the office to George
McComb, who was appointed to the of
fice by the Justices of the Peace.
Ruef contends that an appeal stays all
proceedings, and will, within the next ten
days, file a bill of exceptions to Sloss'
ruling.
McComb's attorneys claim that it is not
an appealable order, arid they are sup
ported In their contention by Judge Sloss,
who, however, has consented to make a
search of the authorities to determine this
new phase of the fight for the office of
Justices' clerk.
In the event that the point raised by
Samuels is found to be correct the order
will be served once. again on Fredrick.
If he refuses to obey; the order then the
execution of the court's dictum will be
placed In the hands of the Sheriff.

Experience has established the ease with
which the great corporations of the State have
at times escaped their Just share of taxes
through the connivance of. friendly boards, ot
equalization. Should this experience be re
peated and this great corporation be able to
avoid Its share of the public burden the weight
of taxation- on 1 the property owners of this
city would necessarily be increased. In any
new franchises are granted pending the de
cision of this matter the risk wlir be incurred
of placing the city street privileges beyond
municipal control, thereby augmenting the
taxes of the people.
The t-an Mateo Railroad has been purchased
by the . United Railroads of San Francisco and
is now part of a system, in fact, as 1 under
stand the question, . the whole system is now
one road. If, therefore, the Supreme Court
should hold that a railroad which operates
in more than one county Is solely within the
jurisdiction of the. State Board of Equalization
in matters of assessment then there will at
once pass from San Francisco the power to
tax this enormous property which has been
developed out of municipal privileges. . '
There is now pending and under submission
in the Supreme .Court .an .action brought to
determine whether the San Mateo electric road
Uhe greater portly of which runs over fran
chises granted hjr this city, but which runs
into San Mateo "County) is not under the law
an intercounty railroad and therefore assess
able exclusively by the State Board of Equal
ization. .

or petroleum to be used as fuel.
The Boards of Works. Health and Fire
Commissioners were instructed to investi
gate the horse yards in the vicinity of
Market and Eleventh streets, and to en
force the ordinance prohibiting the erec
tion of wooden buildings within the fir-a
limits and the sanitary laws governing
euch places of business.
The Board of Works was authorized to
enter into a contract to repave bituminous
streets at a cost of $20,000.
The Board of Works was directed to re
move sixty days from date the frame
building on the sidewaik and roadway of
Jrwin street in front of the property of
"Jiiomas Magee. en Sixth and lrwin
streets.

viding for an amendment to the charter
increasing the salary of the Assessor
iron $4<>*> to f&uuO. The resolution went
to the Charter Amendment .Committee.
A resolution was adopted calling a spe
cial sesfion of the board next Monday at
J Â£0 p. m. lor the purpose -of considering
the advisability ot holding special elec
tions for municipal improvements pro
posed to be acquired through a bond is
sue. The resolution was passed in view
of the opinion of the City Attorney that
the safest course would be to hol.d an
election for each particular class of im
provement.

\\ ilson declared that the subpena was
not competent, and the clerk's communi
cation was ordered to be withdrawn.
A communication from Charles Fay,
clerk of the Board of Supervisors, direct
ing attention to the subpena issued by the
Grand Jury on the members to^ attend Its
session to consider municipal affairs
caused a storm of indignation at yester
day's meeting of the board. The board
took the ground that the Grand Jury had
no right to subptna the members.
"One good turn deserves another," said
DAncona, "'and J move that the clerk of
the board be directed to issue a subpena
to the members of the Grand Jury to ap
pear before this Board of Supervisors on
Tuesday at 2:bO p. m."
"If sora^ people desire to put them
selves in a ridiculous position," said
Comte, "I s*-e no reason why we shouid
do so. I meve that the clerk of tho
board be instructed to ask District At
torney Byington under what law he sub
penas the members of this board to ap
pear before the Grand jury. He has no
i lpnt to command us to appear before the
body."
Brandenstein declared that it was an in
dignity on the board and the District At
torney should be rebuked.
Wynn held that the subpena was served
on him as an individual and that he for
one would attend the Grand Jury's ses
sion.

Governor Heard Urges Passage of
Laws to Successfully Oppose.
the Trusts.
BATON KOUGE, La., May 12.â The bi
ennial session of the Louisiana Legisla
ture began at noon to-day. Among the
matters to come before the body are a re
organization of the judiciary, the reap
pcrtlonment of the State for one addi
tional Congressman and legislation affect
ing the insurance situation in Louisiana
and trusts. Governor Heard presented his
biennial message this afternoon. He rec-
ommends .the appropriation of $100,000 for
a - Louisiana exhibit at the St. Louis Ex
position, and purposes the erection there
of a building reproducing the famous old.
Cabildo, facing Jackson square, where the
cession of the vast Louisiana Territory,
to the Government of the United States
v;aa officially consummated in 1303.
On the subject of trusts the Governor
urges that the laws affecting them be re-'
modeled in accordance with the decisions
of the Federal Supreme Court, in order
that the State may be able to success
fully oppose the combinations, which, he
says, have practically assumed power to
impose special taxes upon the great body
of the people.
FRESNO. May 12. â The' newly organized
Fourth Regiment, Uniform Rank, Knights of
Pythias, has been launched, with F. D. Fisher
of this city . as colonel. Lieutenant Harwka of
Newman as lieutenant colonel, and R. C. Moss
of Kern major of the first battalion. ..-

- BEESWAXâ 27%@29c per lb. ' * C "
Â¦ Additional reports from Sonoma and Santa
Clara counties indicate that the damage to the
Prune crop "will cut It down seriously, though
it will .probably be larger than last --year's
yield, which was very light. It seems that
although the trees blossomed heavily the fruit
failed to set well, owing, it is thought, to
heavy rains during the blooming- period, which
checked pollenizatlon. The Prune growers are
deriving a good deal of consolation, however,
from the conviction that it is more profitable
In dollars and . cents to have a moderate crop
of fine large fruit than. a record-breaking crop
of small; fruit, which was the outlook a month
ago. .

NEW YORK. May 12. â The position of the
local eraser market was undoubtedly the
- source of the depression which ruled in the
track market to-day. This was reinforced by
uie anthracite labor Eituation, as phown by the
suspension of work to-day in the anthracite
region and by fears of a very unfavorable re
port on tiiÂ«- condition of crops on May 1 to 8
made by the Agricultural Department this
afternoon. The statement of the bank* suf
ficiently indicated that some recuperative pro
ws would be used this week. The heavy de
cline of over $C,000.000 in the cash reserves of
the bar.ks had little lifrht thrown upon it to
day, and was a good deal of a puzzle even to
bankers. It was argued that the trust com
panies might be taking Fteps to buiM up their
cash reserve, in accordance with the intimation
from the Cleariryr-hous-e authorities and of cur
rent public discuFf-ion and criticism of the pres
ent financial eituation. The only additional
light on the subject was the report that a
heavy shipment of cash was made to Philadel
phia last week in furtherance of the transfer
of the Choctaw, Oklahoma and Gulf, and which
did not figure in the routine transactions of
the backs. The pressure for funds outside the
stock market was most clearly manifest in the
sterling exchange market, where there was a
violent decline of %c per pound. There were
large offerings cf bankers' bills against further
loans negotiated abroad and London was a free
buyer of stocks during the first nour, thus help-
Ing out the supply of exchange. The statement
of the country's foreign trade for April show
ing- a decline In agricultural products exported
of over $15,000,000 goes to explain the prevail
ing scarcity of exchange' and the persistent
firmness of foreign exchange up to to-day'8
Â¦harp break.
The total pales of etocks for the day,
Â¦which -were not much in excess of half a mil
lion shares, showed that the liquidation was
not lartre or indiscriminate. The stocks which
â¢uffered most seriously also indicate that the
high grade of the property was not an ex
ception. It is recognized that high grade stocks
bave been lifted to prices unwarranted by con
ditions and apparently that the bankers made
a systematic revision and discrimination in
collateral to-day. The consequence was the
melting away of some recently unexplained ad
vance in some of the most substantial etockf
on the list. The coalers had the additional
burden of the etrike to carry. Union Pacific
and Southern Railway were conspicuous for
comparative firmness during the whole day.

XOTXCE to the stockholders of The Risdon
Iron and Locomotive Works Is hereby given
that. In pursuance of a resolution of the
Board of Directors of said corporation,
unanimously adopted at a meeting of said
bo&rd. beld at the office of the corporation.
in the city and county of San Francisco,
State of California, on the 17th day of
March, 1902. a meeting of the stockholders
of The Risdon Iron and Locomotive Works
will he held at the office of the corporation.
No. 29S Steuart Bt., in the city and county of
San Francisco, State of California (the same
being the principal place of business of said
corporation and tha building where the. Board
of Directors usually meet), on WEDNES-
DAY, the 21st day of May. 1902, at the hour
of 11 o'clock a. m.. for the purpose of con-
sidering and acting upon a proposition to
create a bonded indebtedness of said corpo-
ration to the amount of six hundred thou-
sand (600,000) dollars, United States gold
coin, for the purpose of providing moneys
to purchase additional property to complete
the -works and extend the business of the
corporation, and for legitimate and neces-
esxy purposes. By order of the Board of
Directors.
(Signed.) AUGUSTUS TAYLOR,
Secretary of The Risdon Iron and Locomo-
tive Works.

the month has passed. - At the False Alarm
claim a new find was made Thursday by Judge
Massey and Professor Roberts. They ran. a
drift from the shaft to the north and' founl
the ledge in place, "ft Â¦ being- two feet -wide.
This was' not expected,' as a . shaft had been
sunk only a few weeks past and the ledge did
not show, it having been missed by only a few
inches.' A contract was- 'let to the Strauta
brothers yesterday to run fifty feet of shaft
on the ledge. Johnny Vieira, who la mining
north of this claim, has also uncovered what
looks - like a good-paying ledge. He is down
twenty feet and f was about . to give up thft
work when a shot broke Into pay ground. His
property, shows a big ledge and should It con
tinue, to Improve he will have a bonanza in a
short time, as his. ledge is the largest on the
hill. Will Fotherglll and Professor Brannon
have also what appears to be a ledge in their
holdings and they will continue to sink on the
property. - This district is exciting considerable

The secret service authorities are on the
alert for a gang of counterfeiters who 'are
passing a large number tff -bogus .$5 pieces
throughout â¢ the cfty. â¢ The counterfeit,
which bears' the date of 1898, is an almost
perfect imitation of the real coin, only it
is a little off color, being a lighter shade
than the mint product. The downtown
districts have been plentifully supplied
with the bad money, and the authorities
fear that a large supply has already
found its way out of" this city. "A close
watch Is being kept, but so far no arrests
have been made; . ~r Â¦ , " "Â¦

George Bracken. v. - ho came into noto
riety during the recent strike for shooting
a man named Pettit and who has just
ccme out of jail, met a girl named Nel
lie Howard yesterday afternoon and took
her to a saloon at 731 Mission street,
â¢where thev had several drinks. From
there they went to 153^ Third street, and
presumably got into an altercation,
whereupon Bracken struck her on the
head with a bottle and otherwise bruised
her. The woman was taken to the Cen
tral Emergency Hospital, where forty-five
stitches were taken in her head. Bracken
was arrested and taken to the Southern
station, where he was booked on a charge
of assault to murder.

President E. H. Harriman of the South
ern Pacific entertained Governor Odell of
New York last evening at a 'banquet at
the Pacific-Union Club. Sixty prominent
citizens of the State were invited to meet
Governor Odell and the entire party wai
seated at one table.
The decorations for the banquet were
most elaborate." At the end of the dining
room a scene representing a view of
Southern California was depicted,,, while
in the center "of the room a trunk of a
tree was arranged, with an eagle's ne*tV
built on it. ; JJ
President Harriman. as toastmaster. es
! tablished himself on a more friendly toot
i Ing. He opened the banquet in a short
but clever speech, introducing Governor
Udell to the assembled guests.
Governor Odell illustrated the fact that
he was a good after-dinner speaker. In
his remarks he explained that Siate and
municipal politics must.be run on a bus
iness basis. He illustrated hU remarks*
by showing that the last Legislature of
New York State was- the shortest session
in the last 100 years. He concluded hia
speech by paying a globing tribute to Cal
ifornia nospltanty.
Governor Gage followed with a speeqb
in which he said he felt proud that Gov
ernor Odell had paid a visit to Califor
nia and that he felt sure that great ben
efit to the Pacific Coast would follow
from such a distinguished visit. :-. .

Webster Jones is as jovial a chap as
any club man in town who ha3 passed
his forty-year mark. He is a member of
the firm of Jones, Paddock & Co.. the big
coffee and spice people on Fremont street.
This is not his lirst matrimonial venture.
Several years ago he married a Miss
Hobbs, who was one of the most promi
nent society girls in this city. Her father
was a very wealthy man, and, along with
the half million or so that Mr. Jones in
herited at -the death of his. father, the
couple possessed a fortune. that was not
to be sneered at. -But Mr. Jones' matri
monial felicity was short lived. Troubles
came to darken the doors and . soon the
marriage vows were wrecked on the cruel
rocks of divorce. Mrs. Jones entered suit
for divorce in Santa Barbara. Mr. Jones
did not make any contest. Superior Judge
Day of Santa Barbara granted Mrs. Jones
her divorce October 7, 1899. A few months
ago her engagement to'Artsimovitch, the
.then Russian Consul to this city, was
announced. They were married shortly
afterward. "
